Enter the Wrath of the Titans.  As it turned out, I didn't have to write a bad review in order to fall asleep: the movie was bad enough.  Still, some thoughts linger, mainly of how dreadful a movie it was.<br /><br />***SPOILERS, PROBABLY***<br /><br />Wrath of the Titans is one of those films which would not only have no reason to exist were it not for an astonishingly stupid decision made by the hero at the outset, but which is only kept going by a further series of stupid decisions.  Perseus is living a serene, godless existence as a fisherman.  Io (Gemma Arterton from the previous film) has died, leaving Perseus with a son, Helius, who is now a lad.  Zeus comes one evening to tell Perseus that, in the absence of human prayer, the gods' power is diminishing.  This includes the restraints Zeus placed on his father Kronos to keep him imprisoned in Tartarus.  Demons are already breaking free of the underworld to wreak havoc on mankind, but once Kronos escapes it will mean oblivion for gods and men.  Would Perseus please help?<br /><br />\\"The world may be obliterated, but I promised my wife that I'd look after my son,\\" is essentially Perseus's answer.  Consequences be damned, apparently.  Zeus smiles and vanishes.  Later that night, Perseus wakes up freaked out by a dream he had of the world ending.<br /><br />Meanwhile, Zeus meets up with his brother Hades in the underworld.  Zeus's son Ares is also there.  While both are ostensibly present to help keep Kronos in Tartarus, they trap Zeus, whose power they will actually use to free Kronos, who will allow Hades and Ares to keep their immortality.  Why the double-cross?  Well, Hades still resents Zeus for giving him dominion over the underworld, and Ares resents Zeus for preferring Perseus over himself.  Mass destruction therefore makes sense.<br /><br />Perseus changes his mind and goes on a quest with Queen Andromeda (who is even more useless than Perseus) to search for the entrance to Tartarus so he can free his father and defeat Kronos.  Along the way he runs into Hephaestus (played by Bill Nighy, who does that routine of his where he raises his eyebrow, leans forward, blinks, and then lurches back as if he's been spat on, which perhaps is meant to be visual comedy, and probably would have been had he not done it for {whats seems like} every film he's been in) who takes them to the entrance of Tartarus before he's stabbed by Ares, who is able to find them all because one of Andromeda's warriors prayed to him, even though she was quite explicitly told not to lest Ares discover her and kill her, which he does.<br /><br />Anyways, after stumbling through a frustrating rubix-cube of a maze, the gang make it to Zeus.  Perseus attempts to free him.  It's hard going at first, but once Zeus tells him to believe in himself the manacles practically fall away.  Hades has a change of heart at the last minute, putting him at odds with Ares, who's still pissed off.  By now Kronos, a giant of volcanic rock and seething magma, has broken free.  But Ares still finds time to face off with Perseus in a temple atop a mountain, making Helius, whom he has kidnapped, watch the bizarrely erotic spectacle.  Perseus prevails, taking Zeus's thunderbolt (which Ares had stolen) and combining it with Hades's and Poseidon's tridents to form a mega-weapon that he shoves down Kronos throat, bringing on a cosmic bout of acid-reflux, thus annihilating the Titan.<br /><br />Zeus, proud father of demi-god Perseus, crumbles to dust.  Hades does not crumble to dust but says his power is gone anyway.  Perseus gets over it all rather quickly, as evidenced by his putting the moves on Andromeda, who was an old flame of his after all.  It had been ambiguous when she first appeared, making for the only tension in this film.  A tension whichwas, in the end, worthless.<br /><br />It seems Warner Bros. are contemplating a third Titans film, which is strange since they needn't have bothered with this one (or the one before).

B0064MT1SA|5.0|I had a feeling that this would be an exceptional BLU RAY reissue and I'm glad to be proven right.<br /><br />The picture quality is uniformly gorgeous and highlights the vivid array of expertly researched detail the 150-strong production company filled every scene with - Mexican clothing, terracotta interiors, chaotic art studios and colonial South American buildings. Its default aspect is 1.78:1 - so it fills the entire screen without stretching or loss of clarity. You combine this with a sympathetically-written script, brilliant acting and a genuinely affecting and unfolding story (never mind the huge amount of EXTRAS transferred in full from the DVD - see list below) and the whole experience is a joy to re-watch and re-discover.<br /><br />The first thing that strikes you about the print is that 'colour' is everywhere. It opens in Mexico in 1922 when Frida Kahlo is a precocious 15 year-old and able-bodied (before her horrific accident) and over the course of the movie progresses nearly 30 years hence - so lighting - textures - interiors - all have to be matched. The Blues, Reds, Yellows and Gold are full on and evoke a Mexico of the period (all beautifully done by Production Designer Felipe Fernandez - Oscar nominated for his work here).<br /><br />The autobiographical nature of her art is captured in cleverly woven-in scenes and her painful injuries/nightmares are portrayed at times by grotesque animation peopled from her canvas creations. Frida suffered back pain all her life (an iron rod skewered her abdomen and uterus in the accident) and famously painted lying down with a mirror over her bed (she later had toes and a leg amputated due to her injuries). Yet she defied all expectations and after two years in casts managed to walk again. Taymor's movie fills the screen with this - her spirit, her driving need to matter and her bisexual lovelife and gender-bender dress sense. This is a world where politics and passion are seething in the streets - and boozy nights are spent discussing the first and engaging in the second...<br /><br />The cast is large and seriously talented - and Frida is a role Salma Hayek openly admits she'd always wanted to play. The worst you could say about her performance is that her beauty and astonishing sensuality sometimes hinder believability. But she gives her all and her supporting cast are so well chosen that her film-star looks get quickly forgotten. Given the depth and difficulty of the part - her nomination for Best Actress in a Leading Role is richly deserved.<br /><br />And while Ashley Judd (her dialogue discussing their marriage titles this review), Geoffrey Rush, Edward Norton, Antonio Banderas, and Roger Rees all contribute their vignettes with heart and commitment - the film's real ace-in-the-hole is ALFRED MOLINA. He is simply astonishing as Diego Rivera - the Mexican murals painter who married Frida in 1929 at the age of 43 when she was only 22. Diego was a fat ugly man who became her mentor, lover and on/off husband for 25 years. Loveable one minute, loathsome the next - the film is so much better because he's in it (Salma sings his praises in the extras). A lecher, habitual womanizer and a serious-minded Communist - it's a ludicrously difficult part to nail - yet Molina is never anything less than brilliant. Charming at first when he's getting what he wants (wooing Frida in the alley outside his studio) - then to his wandering glassy eyes as infidelity creeps to the surface (watching her sing at a party, but eyeing the other women there) - he later crosses the line completely by sleeping with her sister (fathered a child by her too). The big question is - why did a woman as smart and as committed to loyalty as Frida - countenance this boor for even a second? Friends gave their union 'two months' - her own mother described their marriage as being between \\"...an elephant and a dolphin...\\" The answer the film tells us is LOVE.<br /><br />In interviews, Director Julie Taymor states that both she and Salma returned to Frida's diaries and found they were not about betrayal and men-as-pigs (as many feminists feel) - they were all about Diego and her. Frida really loved him - admired him - supported him. Of course she wanted to slit his throat on several occasions - and was ultimately broken by him (the scene where she cuts her hair is particularly powerful) - but she seemed to stick with his first wife's advice and accept the rough with the smooth because the overall gain was worth it (\\"...He's the best of friends and the worst of husbands.\\"). It's an odd relationship in an oddball world. Yet both Hayek and Molina are so believable that when the film morphs from one of her paintings of the married couple standing in a room into the actors playing them - it's hard to tell the difference. Taymor (who devoted almost two years of her life to the project) does well to rescue Frida from self-pity and loathing - showing instead her courage and joie-de-vive - her fierce loyalty no matter what. Her partner Elliot Goldenthal also provided the film with a beautifully apt Latin guitar score (for which he won the Oscar).<br /><br />To sum up - it was never going to be an easy task to film the life of this icon of Mexican art (Frida) and interpret her tumultuous lifelong relationship with another free soul (Diego) - yet both June Taymor and Salma Hayek pulled it off admirably. It was nominated for six Oscars and won two - Best Make Up and Best Original Music. You can't help but think it should have won more (especially for its two leads)...<br /><br />A quality BLU RAY reissue then - and like a good political knees-up with Leon Trotsky - wholeheartedly recommended.<br /><br />BLU RAY Specifications:<br />VIDEO: 1.78:1 Aspect Ratio [Full Screen]<br />AUDIO: English DTS-HD Master Audio 5.1<br />SUBTITLES: English, English for the Deaf and Hard-Of-Hearing, Spanish<br /><br />EXTRAS:<br />1. (Feature Length) Audio Commentary by Julie Taymor (Director)<br />2. A Conversation With Salma Hayek - Lead Actress Playing Frida Kahlo (near-40 minute facing-camera interview covering all aspects of the shoot)<br />3. AFI (American Film Institute) Q&A with Julie Taymor conducted 2 Oct 2002 by Deszo Magyar (30 minutes)<br />4. Bill Moyers Interview With Julie Taymor (19 minutes)<br />5. Chavela Vargas Interview - A 93-year old Mexican Lady Singer who knew Frida Kahlo and Diego Rivera (Subtitled, 15 Minutes)<br />6. The Voice Of Lila Downs - a Mexican Singer-Songwriter who sang some of the music for the soundtrack (5 minutes)<br />7. The Vision Of Frida with Rodrigo Prieto (Cinematographer) and Julie Taymor (6 minutes)<br />8. The Design of Frida with Felipe Fernandez (Production Designer) (near 3 minutes)<br />9. The Music of Frida with Elliot Goldenthal and Salma Hayek (5 mnutes)<br />10. Salma's Recording Session - she sings \\"La Bruja\\" (near 3 minutes)<br />11. Bringing Frida Kahlo's Life And Art To Film: A Walk Through The Real Locations.<br />Felipe Fernandez talks of his painters, designers and architects who recreated Frida and Diego's home \\"The Blue House\\".<br />They did the courtyard, the vivid wall colours, the cacti, her bed with the mirror above, framed Communist imagery.<br />Later in the film they recreate Diego's modernist home where the couple hosted Trotsky etc (near 6 minutes)<br />12. Portrait Of An Artist - clips from the film mixed with interviews with Julie Taymor, Salma Hayek, Alfred Molina, Geoffrey Rush, Ashley Judd and two Producers (14 minutes)<br />13. \\"Amoeba Proteus\\" - Visual FX Piece (near 10 minutes)<br />14. \\"The Brothers Quay\\" - Visual FX Piece (1 minute)<br />15. Bookmarks - allows you to bookmark portions of the films that both she and Salma returned to Frida's diaries and found they were not about betrayal and men-as-pigs (as many feminists feel) - they were all about Diego and her. Frida really loved him - admired him - supported him. Of course she wanted to slit his throat on several occasions - and was ultimately broken by him (the scene where she cuts her hair is particularly powerful) - but she seemed to stick with his first wife's advice and accept the rough with the smooth because the overall gain was worth it (\\"...He's the best of friends and the worst of husbands.\\"). It's an odd relationship in an oddball world. Yet both Hayek and Molina are so believable that when the film morphs from one of her paintings of the married couple standing in a room into the actors playing them - it's hard to tell the difference. Taymor (who devoted almost two years of her life to the project) does well to rescue Frida from self-pity and loathing - showing instead her courage and joie-de-vive - her fierce loyalty no matter what. Her partner Elliot Goldenthal also provided the film with a beautifully apt Latin guitar score (for which he won the Oscar).<br /><br />To sum up - it was never going to be an easy task to film the life of this icon of Mexican art (Frida) and interpret her tumultuous lifelong relationship with another free soul (Diego) - yet both June Taymor and Salma Hayek pulled it off admirably. It was nominated for six Oscars and won two - Best Make Up and Best Original Music. You can't help but think it should have won more (especially for its two leads)...<br /><br />A quality BLU RAY reissue then - and like a good political knees-up with Leon Trotsky - wholeheartedly recommended.<br /><br />BLU RAY Specifications:<br />VIDEO: 1.78:1 Aspect Ratio [Full Screen]<br />AUDIO: English DTS-HD Master Audio 5.1<br />SUBTITLES: English, English for the Deaf and Hard-Of-Hearing, Spanish<br /><br />EXTRAS:<br />1. B007UQ5Z1G|3.0|It has been awhile since I have written a review of Linkin Park since their previous record \\"A Thousand Suns\\". At that time, it seem that LP was trying to push the boundaries of music to a point of an art form and trying to force the music rather than letting it come naturally. Although the record debut a No. 1 of Billboard it soon fell quickly from the charts because of its lack of depth to the album. Now, in 2012, and the band returns once again with \\"Living Things\\". Now, I have been a huge LP fan since 1998 and have seen their evolution over a course of a decade blending all genres together and I want to recap everything up to this review for those that may be new to their work.<br /><br />From Hybrid Theory to Meteora, they exploded on the scene with ferocity and genre-bending sounds that no one in the \\"Nu-Metal\\" could compete against back in the early 2000s. By 2005, after their success with Jay Z on \\"Collision Course\\", the group wanted to explore and shake the criticism from critics and others alike and placing them in a label that the group collectively didn't want. So, by 2006, they recruited legendary producer Rick Rubin to executive produce the groups third album \\"Minutes to Midnight\\". It was a drastically departure of their recent sounds; Mike Shinoda took a back seat on the project and produce more on the record rather than having his signature rapping on the record (with the exception of \\"Bleed It Out\\" and \\"Hands Held High\\") and push Chester Bennington and rest of the band in the forefront. The album became so controversial that it divided the fan base in half 'til this very day. After the change of the direction of the album the group decided that they wanted to keep pushing the foundation of their work the group follow it up with \\"A Thousand Suns\\" and many people felt that Linkin Park would never get back to their old form. That is, until now....<br /><br />\\"Living Things\\" is a blend of the past along with the continuous exploration of music. Each track seems to have the feel of each of their current work in their catelog being represented to their fans at almost like giving back to the hardcore fan whom have follow them for so long. However there are some slip ups in the record which doesn't make the record perfect:<br /><br />1. Lost in the Echo - 4/5: This is a definite throwback to the days of Meteora. Displaying that Mike Shinoda hasn't lost his touch of rapping after the past two albums. Chester lends a rough and rugged growl to the track along with great production. A great way to start off the album.<br /><br />2. In My Remains - 4/5: Has a great rock sound and Chester brings his signature sound to the track. Mike comes in the bridge with a chant \\"Like an army, forming, one-by-one-by-one\\". Not too bad of a track to follow.<br /><br />3. Burn It Down -4/5: The lead single of the album. It is fitting how Chester started out with the line \\"The Cycle Repeated\\". It almost seems fitting to the thesis of this album. Great production from Mike & Mr. Hahn, almost has that House, Dubstep approach to it. Mike does lend some rapping to the bridge of the song. Overall, has the feel of an older song \\"Blackbirds\\". But, nevertheless, a good record.<br /><br />4. Lies, Greed, Misery - 4/5: This track seems to be the most interesting song of the album. Almost a Fort Minor feel to it as Mike takes control rapping on the track while Chester does the hooks. Pretty good if you have a sound system in your car or on your computer. Still a pretty good track and a possible second single to it.<br /><br />5. Ill Be Gone - 3/5: This is where the record seems to take a course of more and almost melodic side of the group. Not as heavy as previous tracks but still holds good to lyrical prowess. It felt like it needed more substance to the record but this track I really don't seem to care for.<br /><br />6. Castle of Glass - 3/5: I do honestly like this track for the lyrical theme to it. The problem is that Mike Shinoda trying to singon the track. It doesn't fit and throws me off each time I listen to the track. If Chester would have been the person singing on this track I would love it a bit more.<br /><br />7. Victimized - 2/5: The reason I rank this down is the length of the track (1:46). The track is so short and barely has an affect to give like the other tracks on the album. Although, it is great to hear the band as a whole sing the opening chant before Chester growls out the title track. Mike brings in a good rap before the track abruptly ends. Needed to be drawn out a bit more to have an effect.<br /><br />8. Roads Untraveled - 3/5: Similar to the track \\"Castle of Glass\\": good production, almost that bluesy-rock ballad style to the track. The problem: Mike Shinoda singing. His singing is not good for the track and drives me insane even though I do love the message that is brought to the track. Again, Chester needs to dominate the track; it would have been interesting to hear a soulful side of Chester and display that he has various ranges of singing rather than people hearing him scream and growl on many tracks.<br /><br />9. Skin to Bone - 1/5: This is the least favorite track on the album. Each time I try to get myself to listen to the track I just cannot get through it. It doesn't seem to fit on the record. Mike, once again, dominates trying to sing on the track and just doesn't make it a strong feeling to the record. Chester follows in background vocals and the hook but I felt that the production doesn't work along with the selection of who sings and who backs up. I usually skip it.<br /><br />10. Until It Breaks - 4/5: The record picks back up with more of a  funky Hip Hop blend with Mike returning to rapping in what I felt was his best rapping on a Linkin Park record in awhile. Sure, the production felt a bit out of place but it was forgivable. Chester brings in to the bridge and gives a break of the monotony. Then, in surprise, Brad Delson (guitarist) lends his vocals to end the record (this marks the first time that Brad was actually singing vocally on a record).<br /><br />11. Tinfoil - 5/5: OMG, for those that love Hybrid Theory & Meteora they remember certain tracks that were dedicated to Mr. Hahn production breakdown. Well, its back! Although short in length, it is refreshing to hear the breakdown.<br /><br />12. Powerless - 4/5: The album ends off with a pretty good track that Chester shows a good strong rock ballad. Production is good and holds up for Chester and flows thoroughly throughout the track.<br /><br />Overall, the album is a lot better than the previous two albums. For those that harp over them not recreating the days of Hybrid Theory and Meteora, this is as close as everyone is going to get to those albums. But, its great for them to combine their previous work and still trying to push the foundations of music. Although, I still feel that places like Mike trying to sing needs to be cut down or ended completely, allowing him to rap a bit more and produce more and leave those tracks to Chester or even Brad. Yet, production seems to be all over the place and tracks seem to not flow through seamlessly like older albums. But, it is a start in the right direction for the group. For the hardcore LP fans, pick up this album and give it a shot. For those new to LP, have a listen to tracks like \\"Burn It Down\\" and \\"Lies, Greed, Misery\\".

0805209999|5.0|On Trial is Josef K. a respected banker in an unnamed city. He faces charges that are never explained to him and about which he gains little information. He vainly tries to mount a defense, much to the detriment of his work, using the few resources available to him and his family. Along the way he alienates some who might further help him and ultimately ends up trying to fight the charges alone. The trial coupled with his work exhaust Josef K. and by the end of the book does not protest his final sentence. Throughout the novel the reader comes to the conclusion that the trial itself is not for any specific thing Josef K. has done but rather his fitness a human being. In essence he is on trial for how well he may be able to mount a defense. When first faced with the charges Josef K. is defiant and confident of acquittal but over a period of time he comes to accept his fate. This is well illustrated in the climax of the novel in which Kafka presents a parable to explain Josef K.'s situation. Kafka thoroughly convinces the reader of exactly what they have to fear.<br /><br />I want to stress there are many ways to interpret this book. Whether as a commentary on justice or an existentialist parable Kafka has much to offer the reader. I would implore anyone who is considering reading Kafka to pick up this novel it is well worth a read.

B00782O7IY|5.0|\\"The Artist\\" is a 2011 motion picture about the end of the era of silent films and the birth of the talkies. Filmed in luxurious black-and-white and featuring classy title cards on-screen as a throwback to the original silent film genre, this newest production perfectly captures the pristine and amazing look of a by-gone time in cinematic history. Upon its initial release, it recieved universal acclaim from film critics and movie-goers alike. Produced on a modest budget of $15 million, it went on to earn a staggering amount of nearly $134 million during its first-run theatrical showings. The film was also awarded international accolades from the film community, dominating Best Picture prizes at the Cannes Film Festival, Golden Globes and Oscars, among others. The overall end result is a remarkable masterpiece and an instant classic. It was the first French film to ever win Best Picture (Thomas Langmann, Producer); the first mainly silent film to win since 1927's \\"Wings\\" (the first recipient of the Best Picture Award, in 1929); the first film presented in the 4:3 aspect ratio to win since 1955's \\"Marty\\"; the first black-and-white film to win since 1993's \\"Schindler's List\\"; and the first non-R-rated film to win since 2004's \\"Million Dollar Baby\\". It also earned Oscars for Michel Hazanavicius for Best Director, Jean Dujardin as Best Actor, as well as Best Costume Design and Best Original Score.<br /><br />The idea of introducing a silent movie to a new generation of movie audiences here in the 21st century is a major accomplishment that succeeds even beyond the aspirations of the filmmakers. The end result is astonishing and breathtaking, while remaining true to the art of film-making that exemplifies everything that there is to enjoy about going to a local movie theater and experiencing filmed images projected onto the silver screen. \\"The Artist\\" was released in the U.S. on DVD and Blu-ray on 26 June 2012. Both variations also include an ultra-violet digital copy of the film. It was released in the UK on 28 May 2012.<br /><br />Looking for something completely different in the ever-changing world of cinema? B0071XO8RA|5.0|I downloaded the \\"Wool\\" omnibus edition to my Kindle a few months ago. It sat there until I finished Hugh Howey's previous work, the various adventures of Molly Fyde - a four-book series that was, quite simply, astounding. If anything, Wool is an even better read. Howey originally presented it as a series of five closely related short stories. The omnibus edition combines these stories and it reads like a full-fledged novel. Howey is fairly new at the novelist's game but he is a talent to be reckoned with. All of his books have grabbed me by the throat and pulled me into his wonderfully-constructed worlds.<br /><br /> Our story begins in a post-apocalyptic America - approximately 300-400 years in the future. We are introduced to a series of characters that live in a subterranean silo of massive proportions. Humans have lived in this silo for generations and live a highly structured and severely limited lifestyle. Since this is all they've known for generations, most of the people accept it and go about their lives. Outside the silo is a toxic wasteland - to venture outside is a death sentence. In fact, people who do express a curiosity about what's outside can be evicted; a certain death sentence. So, talk of the outside is strictly forbidden.<br /><br /> The character development is first-rate. I learned to love nearly all of them - especially Juliette. Juliette makes this story totally rock.<br /><br /> As with any dystopian story, things go wrong and they go wrong in a very spectacular fashion. As events begin to unravel, our various protagonists - and the necessary antagonists - fall into a series of mind-boggling adventures and conflicts. When I wasn't reading this book, I was thinking about it and cutting corners so I could get back to it as soon as possible. When I finished it, I was disappointed. Why, you may ask? Well, I wanted the story to continue. Even with a small measure of resolution at the end of the book, the characters and the many obvious challenges facing them simply begs for more story. The possibilities - yes, even in a limited silo area - are virtually endless. When you read this story, you'll understand this and you'll understand it well. The author has indicated he will continue this story - and I hope he's serious about doing so.<br /><br />I read somewhere that Ridley Scott bought the film rights to this book. If true, I do hope he sees his way to film it - it would make a great movie. Scott would certainly be the best choice to produce and direct it - although I think James Cameron could also do it justice.<br /><br />There is so much this review will not tell you. To say too much is to give away a wonderful plot line full of twists and turns and wholly unexpected discoveries. No spoiler here - but I hope this review alone is sufficient to cause the reader to buy Howey's book: \\"Wool - the Omnibus Edition\\"
B0071XO8RA|5.0|Wool took me by surprise. I knew, after reading other reviews, that it would deal with a grim, dystopian futuristic world. That there was something about humanity, what was left of it, being trapped inside a huge underground complex dubbed the \\"silo\\", condemned to survive there as the outside had become unfit for life. That sometimes, even conscious of signing their own death certificates, people inside the silo wanted out. And were granted their wish. Apart from that, I knew nothing.<br />And that's exactly why reading Wool was such an extraordinary experience. Spoiling such a book would be a big mistake, and even the smallest hint could be considered as a spoiler. Though this is terribly frustrating, I really believe I can't say too much and you must trust all the five-star reviews you've seen, and all the good comments you've heard. The storyline is great, the style is awesome too, and the pages fly by, even though this story is not \\"easy\\". By that I mean you don't have whole pages of dialogues, or empty paragraphs. Everything is deep (pun intended), gritty and filled with important, recurring themes, making Wool not only an amazing sci-fi story, but also a real literary achievement.<br />I've read comparisons to Herbert, Asimov and others. Reading Wool's Book one, I was more reminded of George Orwell's 1984, which is the best compliment I can make, as I consider it one of the most seminal books ever. The two novels share common themes such as control of the population through transformation or erasure of its past, constant pressure, fear, punishment. The verticality of the silo also epitomizes the symbolical social divides at work in any society. Lie and deceit are omnipresent, you can never know what's true or not, you can never believe anything at face-value. As a reader, I was as misled as the characters, believed I had cracked it and was dead wrong. Amazing stuff.<br />Another comparison imposed itself in my mind toward the end of Book 1, which is very short. I experienced the same longing I felt while watching the series Lost: very quickly, you're hooked and puzzled at the same time, and that's why you read so fast. In Wool, 1984 and Lost, lie and deceit are constant, as well as a need to escape from something quickly becoming unbearable. And you want to see who will make it. Funny how a majority escapes vicariously, through the more concrete escape of others leading to death: the scapegoats (the paradoxical sentence befalling outlaws is being sent OUT, and it's called \\"cleaning\\", turning Wool into a reenactment of the Witch Hunt at times). Each party blinded by their own personal faiths and beliefs. Funnily enough, wool is the material used to clean the camera lenses outside so dwellers in the silo can still have a view, but wool can also be put on one's eyes so he can't see... There's a constant back and forth between positive and negative in Wool, a tug-of-war between life and death, despair and hope. Just as there's a constant movement up and down the silo, down leading to the depths of hell, up leading to its threshold...<br />There's an immediate feeling of absoluteness when reading Wool, the sense that you're holding something important in your hands, a legend rooted in biblical references (the Tower of Babel, except here the highest levels don't quite reach Heaven, but only Earth, and an Earth that's getting to look like Hell, at that), and sprouting its limbs in modern tales (I remember reading a strange manga entitled \\"Blame!\\", in which the hero had to travel up and down a giant vertical subterranean structure too) . It's an instant classic mixing many things and creating a new one, poetic, strong and mystical. Why three times forty eight stories in the silo? Is it to be read 3:48: \\"Streams of tears flow from my eyes because my people are destroyed\\"? Is the Mayor, Marie Jahns, a new weeping prophet like Jeremiah, like him feeling a deep and abiding passion for her people and their city? I may be going too far, here. But at the same time, why not 150 stories, then? And why a choice of name so close to an anagram?<br />You tend to lend the characters that mythical potential, so extremely well-defined, strong and symbolical are they. Good or bad, they immediately leave a mark in your brain. They all stand for something, and as with the rest in this novel, there's nothing gratuitous about what they say or do.<br />OK, I need to stop. I could go on for hours. Just go and buy the damn book, because it really is AWESOME.

0441013597|4.0|So much has been written about Frank Herbert's \\"Dune\\" in the nearly 50 years since its publication that it's difficult for the modern reader to come at the novel with expectations that are even remotely realistic. As for readers like me -- I've probably read the book half a dozen times by now, if not more -- I'm sometimes not entirely sure what I think of it myself. At times, it can seem very pretentious to me, and there are occasions, when revisiting it, that the precious, overweening dialogue drives me straight up the wall. But when I return to it with an open mind, I re-experience what drove me through it on my first reading: The sense of awe, the marvel of it, the magnificence and ambition of its narrative.<br /><br />\\"Dune\\" truly is an epic novel, not just because it's more than 500 pages long. It's the scope of the book that resonates with readers, I think -- this is a \\"science fiction\\" novel that's also about religion, mysticism, race memory, politics, ecology, warfare and diplomacy. It's a huge book, but it doesn't wander, nor does it get mired down in its many themes.<br /><br />Thankfully, the story is strong, which keeps the book moving. Paul Atreides may or may not be the new messiah, the young man who, according to prophecy, will lead the Fremen of Arrakis to dominate the known Universe. His father, Duke Leto Atreides, is murdered by the Harkonnens -- royal cousins, yes, but also sworn enemies of House Atreides. It falls to Paul to explore the potential of the desert-dwelling Fremen, even as he begins to experience his own powers of prescience and considerable gifts for leadership. Long oppressed by the Harkonnens, the Fremen are only too glad to accept a young leader willing to upend not just the politics of the planet, but of all humanity.<br /><br />Herbert's storytelling genius was matched by his larger vision. Clearly, water-starved Arrakis is a metaphor for our own world's dependance on oil. But it turns out that \\"Dune\\" was literally about water, too -- that is, Herbert realized when he was writing it that water would become, over time, an increasingly precious substance. All you have to do these days to realize he was right is pay attention to the news -- news of droughts, yes, but also water rights being bought up by companies looking to profit at a situation some in our culture see as increasingly alarming.<br /><br />\\"Dune's\\" other great theme -- and warning -- is its exploration at the apparently universal human desire for a messiah-like figure to emerge and provide hope, wisdom and guidance. As \\"Dune\\" reveals, such a desire has its dark side. Paul Atreides transformation into Paul-Maud'dib comes with a terrible price, both to himself and, ultimately, to his people.<br /><br />Fiction writers are supposed to create worlds in which we can immerse ourselves, worlds that seem as real as our own, at least while we are turning those pages. Science fiction writers, especially, have the task of creating entire alternate worlds that nevertheless seem believable, even plausible. While some of the science in \\"Dune\\" is rather \\"soft\\" compared with other novels in the genre, it really doesn't matter. After all these readings, I'm still not exactly sure how, for example, a Guild Heighliner achieves faster-than-light speed, except that it \\"folds\\" space, and requires a navigator who has at least some ability to see into the future. Beyond that, Herbert doesn't offer much in the way of explanation. No worries; you'll be carried along by the author's enthusiastic narrative drive.too -- that is, Herbert realized when he was writing it that water would become, over time, an increasingly precious substance. B007OWG3LM|5.0|It doesn't come as a surprise the critics didn't receive Valtari well. Its sound is too subtle to be picked apart fairly in a first impression. The only reason I gave the album as concerted effort as I did was because of my respect for the group and relief in their return to their classic style. Otherwise I would have only given it as much time as the critics did: enough to see that there's nothing new, no loud revelations, and only so much reflection on their past success. The four years aged their style past its peak of flavor, so to speak.<br /><br />Unlike Agaetis Byrjun and Takk... there is no outright moment of conversion when the group's artistic style bows to its new prince. There's no single song on Valtari that can stand alone like Hjarta Hamast or Glsli. You experience from song to song a steady widening that slowly inflates the boundaries of what they've built in the past. Adding nothing, Valtari spreads the old pieces around in a dark room waiting for the participant to fumble his way to them and lay his hands on them. I think this is what the New York Times' Jon Pereles, the most apt of the Valtari critics, meant when he wrote that \\"each of the new songs looms within a larger, obsessively detailed ambience . . . as if the band's old pure musical sanctuary has been overgrown and started to crumble, with different light and air glinting through the cracks,\\" except that I don't agree the softening is really like the passage of time. This is just a positive spin on the critics' observation. To show this I'll merit his metaphor with an extension. The sanctuary was always overgrown and crumbling, but Agaetis and ( ) were so deep inside it you wouldn't know. Takk was really the first sight of washed out light seeping in. Valtari has tentatively emerged.<br /><br />You can even note a difference among Sigur Rs fans, too. Valtari has separated the sheep from the goats. (As a clue to who is who, it's significant that one bandmember has said Valtari is the only album he'll sit down and listen to.) This is a work that demands your time and patience, and the half of us just waiting for flair give up on it. I listened to the album twice (without breaks) with the expectation of just familiarizing myself. Music usually grips, excites, or fractures me. Each listen, my experience came up just a little short of what I wanted, and I deferred my \\"real\\" listen to the future. Before my third time I recognized it was the moment, and this time I heard it as something from the past, maybe jumping over the present to the future, but certainly not something existing then at that hour I could affect intellectually. After about fifteen minutes I stopped thinking about the music, and I felt like a stone had been dropped into the surface of my mind as into a lake, and I observed, in medias res, the chords and harmonies as ripples spreading out smoothly within me. It was a calming word: \\"You are capable of waiting; you waited for this.\\" I have never been so abundantly awarded by contemporary music. Valtari hasn't gotten over the old albums. It has consummated them.<br /><br />I suppose that's why they say this is the classical music of the twenty-first century. At the same time, this is a vignette, not a masterpiece. I think even those other fans defending it will agree with me on this one. You do have to hear the album end-to-end, and it operates more as an artistic moment than a narrative or epic. I think that's a good thing. Jonsi has said enough pretentious things in the past to make me believe they'd attempt the latter too soon. But they may be prepared now, with Valtari as groundwork. Whether or not Sigur Rs need a long haitus like their inspiration, Arvo Prt, in order to achieve that, their flock will wait to see.

0439023513|5.0|I just finished Mockingjay literally 10 minutes ago and like a lot of reviewers here, I feel a little sad and hollow. I agreed with much of the review in question, but wholeheartedly disagree that the ending is hopeless- devoid of any promise of future happiness and filled with the idea that the war was fought in vain. I don't find that to be the case at all, in fact it couldn't be further from the truth. Yes, Katniss was our heroine- the one we followed, and cried for, the one we loved and hoped for. Peeta as well, and Gale and Cinna, Finnick, Annie, Boggs, Rue, Haymitch, Prim... We loved all these people very much. But what you have to accept in reading these books is that you are reading a story of a horrible, bloody WAR- where more life will be lost than saved. But this was a war for FUTURE independence, for FUTURE hope. The war was being fought so that one day, perhaps good people like Katniss and Peeta could be allowed to have children who wouldn't starve to death in the street or kill eachother in the Hunger Games. That was the whole point of the books... That the people we grew to love had to suffer, and continued to suffer in the battle to bring about a brighter, better future where hopefully, the atrocities we painfully watched them face would not continue into further generations. And from the ending we were given, it is clear that the battle was won. The atrocities have not continued, but ceased. While Katniss, Peeta and all the others we loved are perhaps horribly, irreparably changed by the horrors they faced in order to bring about this safer, more hopeful new world- I like to think of their children. That other reviewer was right when she said that Katniss seemed somewhat lacking in hope, but we were with her through everything. From reliving her father's death, to being ripped from her family and tortured in nightmarish arenas, being forced to become a killer, losing her home, never being allowed to trust, down to the murder of the girl that was the most precious thing in the world to her. That the Mockingjay even survived all of this is the only miracle we can expect. The fact that she ended up realizing her love for Peeta and gave him two children who will thrive and be allowed to prosper and be happy in the world their parents sacrificed everything to create for them? This is the greatest gift of all. Bottom line- the characters we loved from the beginning were born into doom and any wise reader should have seen that to turn it all around was going to require their entire hearts and souls, if not their very lives. So they gave them up. Katniss did. Peeta did. Prim did. Finnick did. Haymitch did. Gale did. They all did. But now Peeta and Katniss's children are playing... Happy. They're free. Bottomest line- OUR people? Irrefutably changed/damaged/killed by war. But the people of our people? The next generations?  They ARE hope personified. So don't give me this bs about \\"no hope\\" because that is the only thing to celebrate at the end of this AMAZING trilogy. We lost so much. But hope lives on. It was all for the glory of a greater future. B007P6VLRS|5.0|The latest offering from the crankiest rock star of the '90s, Billy Corgan, may very well be the best he's had to offer in over a decade, but it's been a long and windy road getting there. First, there was the triumphant 2005 announcement of a Pumpkins reunion in the wake of a string of failed side-projects which turned out to merely be Corgan and long-time drummer Jimmy Chamberlain as a two-man band. The two produced and released \\"Zeitgeist\\" in 2007 to lukewarm reception. Almost immediately upon impact, the mainstream rejected it, critics bashed it and fans felt bitter and jaded. It wasn't long before Corgan was lashing out at his audience during the band's troubled 20th anniversary tour and Chamberlain bailed out of the group once and for all, leaving Corgan to his own devices. After years of tinkering around with a massive 44-song album dubbed \\"Teargarden By Kaleidyscope\\" and distributing the first quarter or it online, the bald alt-rock anti-hero finally settled on a line-up and decided it was time to return to the traditional album format. Thus, The Smashing Pumpkins are reborn. Again.<br /><br />It's safe to say that at this point, most people have written Corgan and the Smashing Pumpkins brand off, so it's no surprise that the 13 passages of \\"Oceania\\" strive to prove something to the listener. Whether that \\"something\\" is that Corgan can still rock it like the old days or that the newer, younger band is every bit as competent as the old, classic line-up is completely plausible. One thing is for sure, though: \\"Oceania\\" rocks in ways nobody could have foreseen. Calling to mind the band's debut from nearly 25 years (!) ago, \\"Gish,\\" \\"Oceania\\" is a sprawling rock epic that brings enough showy musicianship and flare to crush any and all indie pretenses. Put frankly, Billy Corgan came here to rock, whether you like it or not. Even on softer, synth-heavy tunes like \\"Violet Rays,\\" where he threatens to \\"kiss anyone tonight,\\" Corgan appears to have a very clear and defined mission.<br /><br />One of the main troubles of \\"Zeitgeist\\" was that it was over-produced and entirely too loud (pre-dating Metallica's bid for Loud War dominance by a year). Thankfully, the sound of \\"Oceania\\" is quite a bit easier on the ears, allowing the dynamics to come through in the mix. That means the bass-licks of Nicole Fiorentino actually matter and serve to define certain moments of the album just as much as D'Arcy did in the \\"Mellon Collie\\" days. Likewise, Chamberlain's replacement, a young drum prodigy by the name of Mike Byrne, is given a spotlight on more than a few occasions, keeping the signature Pumpkins sound alive almost effortlessly. Meanwhile, Corgan and his right-hand man Jeff Schroeder weave in and out of each other without steeping on one another's toes. Nowhere is this chemistry more apparent than on the opening anthem, \\"Quasar.\\" Unlike \\"Zeitgeist\\" which very much felt like Corgan controlling everything, \\"Oceania\\" is a much more organic experience brought to life by a real band with personality and chops to boot.<br /><br />While there may be no radio-hits ala \\"Today\\" or \\"Bullet With Butterfly Wings\\" to propel \\"Oceania\\" into the pop-culture stratosphere, it's likely to win back a lot of burnt-out fans and prove a lot of critics wrong. As one of the few alt-rockers with a genuine appreciation and understanding of his instrument, it's inspiring to hear Corgan playing nice with his band-mates for once. \\"Oceania\\" is everything a Pumpkins album should be; it's focused, balanced, organic and mostly importantly:  it flat-out rocks. If you think you've already heard all the classics Corgan has to offer, think again. There's an ocean's worth of epic material to swim through in \\"Oceania.\\"ned mission.<br /><br />One of the main troubles of \\"Zeitgeist\\" was that it was over-produced and entirely too loud (pre-dating Metallica's bid for Loud War dominance by a year). Thankfully, the sound of \\"Oceania\\" is quite a bit easier on the ears, allowing the dynamics to come through in the mix. That means the bass-licks of Nicole Fiorentino actually matter and serve to define certain moments of the album just as much as D'Arcy did in the \\"Mellon Collie\\" days. Likewise, Chamberlain's replacement, a young drum prodigy by the name of Mike Byrne, is given a spotlight on more than a few occasions, keeping the signature Pumpkins sound alive almost effortlessly. Meanwhile, Corgan and his right-hand man Jeff Schroeder weave in and out of each other without steeping on one another's toes. Nowhere is this chemistry more apparent than on the opening anthem, \\"Quasar.\\" Unlike \\"Zeitgeist\\" which very much felt like Corgan controlling everything, \\"Oceania\\" is a much more organic experience brought to life by a real band with personality and chops to boot.<br /><br />While there may be no radio-hits ala \\"Today\\" or \\"Bullet With Butterfly Wings\\" to propel \\"Oceania\\" into the pop-culture stratosphere, it's likely to win back a lot of burnt-out fans and prove a lot of critics wrong. As one of the few alt-rockers with a genuine appreciation and understanding of his instrument, it's inspiring to hear Corgan playing nice with his band-mates for once. \\"Oceania\\" is everything a Pumpkins album should be; it's focused, balanced, organic and mostly importantly:  it flat-out rocks. If you think you've already heard all the classics Corgan has to offer, think again. There's an ocean's worth of epic material to swim through in \\"Oceania.\\"

B007Y6OZE0|4.0|I bought an early release of this album on June 21st, so I have had enough time to let this album sink and this is easily a great new album from the Offspring. is it perfect? no, but I think it is far above what a lot of reviewers rate. I have come to realize through various internet reviews that there are 2 main extreme negative types of complaints about the Offspring's new album.<br /><br />1.) The people that are personally offended, angered and \\"totally done with the Offspring forever man!!\\" because of Cruising California (Bumpin' In My Trunk) and OC Guns.<br />2.) The people that hate it because \\"who cares? sounds like all the other stuff they ever did.\\"<br /><br />I am going to disregard those 2 jaded, childish views and rate this song by song based on the way I have come to perceive the Offspring over the years.<br /><br />1.)The Future Is Now-When I first popped the disc in, this song was a great intro to the album. High energy, In your face politically charged lyrics and powerful instruments. Yes, it does sound like some Rise Against song(thanks youtube!), but one big difference. I like Dexter's voice. I cannot stand Tim McIlrath's voice. As for this song, it is still good, nowhere near the best on the disc, but good opener. It kind of grew off me after a few listens and flatlined. good, but not great. maybe it will un-flatline with time (3/5)<br /><br />2.)Secrets From the Underground - Maintains a lot of the serious tone and energy of the first song, however this one has more replay value and is more interesting to me. I feel that this one could easily fit in on Conspiracy Of One or Rise and Fall Rage and Grace. Once again it is pretty good, but not the best one the album. Could still grow on me more possibly. (3.5/5)<br /><br />3.)Days Go By - The lead single from which the album is named after. It is a good song, with a pretty boring music video. At this point in the Offspring's mainstream career, it is hard not to expect a song like this to pop up.  The song had to grow on me once again. Its a keeper though (3.5/5)<br /><br />4.)Turning Into You - Its hard not to be reminded of Rise Against on this song when Dexter starts singing. The chorus is strong and I feel it saves the song from being a mediocre halt. Sounds like a song from their stagnant Splinter album. Not horrible, but one of the lesser songs on this album.(2.75/5)<br /><br />5.)Hurting As One - Now this is where the album starts picking up a lot. This is the first song on this album that I could comfortably call a punk song. Sounds like Smash/Ixnay era. no joke! If Gone Away and Something To Believe in had a child, it would be this song. It is great to see something like this wash away the stagnancy of the previous track. This song shows us the still great punk band hiding under the mainstream rock band they have morphed into. By the end of this song, I'm excited for the rest of the album! Maybe there are more surprises to follow, eh? (5/5)<br /><br />6.)Cruising California (Bumpin' In My Trunk) - Now this is where I completely disconnect from most of the popular opinion. I completely understand that this is a satirical song poking fun at the abysmal mainstream pop radio. This is the light-hearted pop punk Offspring that made Pretty Fly and Want You Bad. It sounds like the classic pop punk Offspring of the late 90's/early 2000's musically. Also, this song is strategically placed to break up the monotonous \\"serious\\" tone of the album. If you take this one seriously, the joke's on you.(4/5)<br /><br />7.)All I Have Left Is You - Sloooooow song time, but I don't hate slow songs done by certain bands and genres of music. The thing is, the Offspring aren't great at slow, sappy ballads like this. If you liked Fix You from the last album, this may be your thing. To be fair, the song picks up some steam and starts getting good as it gets louder and the lyrics are touching, but to me this is one of the lesser tracks.(2.5/5)<br /><br />8.)OC Guns - Another California song. This one deserves Cruising California's criticisms. An obvious joke song. A reggae/ska-ish song. Fun to hear once. Low point of the album. Rating is for these guys giving reggae/ska a shot. (2/5)<br /><br />9.)Dirty Magic - Re-working of the classic Ignition track. sounds similar, except backing vocals in the chorus. Still as excellent as it has always been. Great to hear a classic. (5/5)<br /><br />10.)Secret Family - pop punk song. pretty good, but not great. sounds like a Rise and Fall Rage and Grace outtake. (3/5)<br /><br />11.)Dividing By Zero - Yes! back to classic badass Offspring. You can hear some Americana mixed with self-titled Offspring in this one. Fast, quick, and to the point.  More like this! (5/5)<br /><br />12.)Slim Pickens - Last song and they end it with a bang! This and the previous track are the best tracks on this album. This was the Offspring I wanted to have on the entire album right here. This is a definite song that could have come from Americana. Superb ending! (5/5)<br /><br />All in all, the album itself is easily the best since Conspiracy of One since it is the first since that album to actually give us a glimpse of that great hard rocking punk band that released all those classics in the 90's. While it is not perfect, it is definitely better than the 2003 trainwreck called Splinter and the pretty good, but not great Rise and Fall album. over half of the album is easily worth owning if your a fan of punk or 90's alternative. Let's hope that their next album is More Slim Pickens, Dividing By Zero, Hurting As One and Less Turning Into you and Future is now.

1604591625|4.0|Unlike many books on apologetics, Chesterton does not present carefully crafted arguments using e logical method. Instead he examines the paradoxes and everyday experiences of life and claims that Christianity better explains life than alternate worldviews. His ideas are more a collections of life's observations rather than a carefully crafted polemic. Chesterton does have a flair for language and uses many analogies to illustrate his points. If you are looking for a book to quote there are many good quotable points in this book. Note that the title of the book is orthodoxy but don't expect very much defense of any specific orthodox Christian doctrine. This is more of a general apologetic with a well-written and very literary style

B007L6VR12|5.0|IN A WORLD WHERE films must shock, where films must employ impossible stunts, extreme violence and outrageous characters, comes Moonrise Kingdom - with none of it. This is a real STORY, with real characters, in which everything that happens is actually possible. What a concept. Told with humor and compassion, the film sweeps you along without the dreaded need for suspension of disbelief. The story is pure Americana - life, liberty and the pursuit of happiness. Some do it better than others. Not everyone is a winner. Its stars - seven of them! - are all totally out of character. This is not a Bill Murray vehicle or an Edward Norton vehicle or a Bruce Willis vehicle. The stars fit in, almost as afterthoughts. It's the story that counts. Most impressively, it has style. From the opening shots of Susie, face on with her binoculars in various rooms of the house, you know this film will be stylish. And understated. It is worthy of any screen in the world, in a world where American films have become overpoweringly boring and repetitive and entirely predictable. This is the kind of film that France would have originated, and which Americans would have remade into a noxious overkill of action and adventure. It is therefore rare and precious. It will endure long after Transformers XXVII has faded. Kudos to Wes Anderson and team for resisting the urges that every other Hollywood production takes for a granted as a requirement. Bob Balaban as the narrator, making his own appearance in the story, is inspired, as is his framing in the shots. That kind of attention to style gives Moonrise Kingdom the ability to sweep you away. On these shores, this is the first must see in years.

B00012QM8G|5.0|It is said in the Talmud, the central text for Judaic law and ethics, that whoever saves one life saves the world entire. That is certainly true of Oskar Schindler, a German industrialist who defied the Nazis to save the lives of 1,200 Jews. But as \\"Schindler's List\\" tells us, he was not always morally compassionate for the Jews.<br /> Schindler saw putting Jews to work in his factory as more of a \\"Well, I can't make money any other way. But I can protect them from Hitler.\\" He wore a swastika on his jacket only to gain respect and profits from the Nazi party. You can say that by wearing a swastika and receiving benefits from the Nazis, Schindler was himself a Nazi. But it also comes down to the fact that he didn't bring harm to the Jews. Steven Spielberg, the director of \\"Schindler's List\\", has said that Schindler was less of a Nazi in his heart. He was just a greedy businessman who saw the Jews as a way to make money, and only later did he come to care for the Jews.<br /> I'm sure that most people back in 1993 were shocked that Spielberg made this movie with the amount of violence that it shows and offers. In previous pictures such as \\"Indiana Jones and the Last Crusade\\", Spielberg cut the Nazis down into treasure hunters and less of what they really were. But in \\"Schindler's List\\", he shows us the true meaning of a Nazi. Most of them were faceless soldiers who mercilessly massacred not just Jews, but millions of other innocent human beings. Now why I do say most? Weren't all the Nazis savage animals?<br /> History has showed us time and time again that within a large army of animalistic humans, there is always at least one person who knows what's right. Schindler was that morally incorruptible being. Spielberg shows us that his turning point in the film is the liquidation of the Krakow Ghetto, where he witnesses on horseback the gathering up of Jews to be sent away to the Nazi death camps. He begins to feel compassion for people who could not defend themselves, and he knew what he had to do.<br /> It is Liam Neeson's performance as Schindler that was most satisfying about the character. Schindler had already been adapted for the screen incredibly well, and Neeson was the human link that made the performance great, if not brilliant.<br /> The Nazis, particularly Amon Goeth (in a remarkable performance by Ralph Fiennes), were selfish, malevolent beings who sought world conquest and the destruction of people who were different. Spielberg shows us that they loved murdering innocent people. They got great pleasure in doing it. If it had been any other way, \\"Schindler's List\\" would have been an example of a Holocaust parody.<br /> One notable performance that is consistently overlooked is by Ben Kingsley as Itzhak Stern, a Jewish accountant whom Schindler recruits to be his right hand man. Stern criticizes Schindler at multiple moments in the film. He believes that some of Schindler's tactics are not successful in rescuing human beings. At other times, he praises Schindler for being incorruptible and willing to risk his own life to save hundreds of others.<br /> Another key element of \\"Schindler's List\\" is the violence of the picture. Spielberg doesn't turn the violence and hatred into things of fantasy. He shows people being shot in the back of the head, being incinerated, and being lead to what seems to be the gas chambers (water comes out miraculously instead of gas). As I stated before, the Nazis took great pleasure in killing Jews.<br /> I saw the film again today for the second time in my life, and this time around I remembered why I love this film so much. Seeing it again, I examined \\"Schindler's List\\" as a film that preserves history. Spielberg brings together all of his resources and all the \\"Schindler Jews\\" that he could to get the story straight and accurate. If he had followed a path that lead to generic melodrama, it would have been extremely offensive. (But he obviously knew that.)<br /> At the end of the movie, we are shown the second shots of color (the first being the very beginning of the film with a family celebrating the Sabbath) as we see the \\"Schindler Jews\\" in present-day place rocks on Schindler's grave. We see each of their names as they place the rocks on the grave. The last shot of the movie is of Liam Neeson standing over Schindler's grave. It's a powerful sight.<br /> But examining \\"Schindler's List\\" for the second time, I began to think about the detractors of the movie. Claude Lanzmann, the director of the 9  hour documentary \\"Shoah\\", called \\"Schindler's List\\" a kitschy melodrama and a deformation of the truth, as he wanted to be the only voice in the true account of the Holocaust. The claim he made is extraordinarily inappropriate and offensive. Anyone who wants to show the Holocaust truthfully should be allowed to, and Spielberg pulled it off.<br /> Stanley Kubrick has said that \\"the Holocaust was about 6 million Jews who got killed. \\"Schindler's List\\" is about 600 who don't.\\" In truth, \\"Schindler's List\\" uses the Holocaust as a basis for the story. Roger Ebert has said that the film is about character studies, in terms of Oskar Schindler and Amon Goeth, and he is right. If the film had been about the Holocaust, I don't think that Schindler's name would have even been brought up. As much as I admire the films of Stanley Kubrick, I must say that he was flat-out wrong in terms of \\"Schindler's List.\\"<br /> So what is the movie about? If I were asked to answer that question, I would say that \\"Schindler's List\\" is about defying the greatest of all evil in the greatest of all perils. It is not about the Holocaust. It is one man's story, and continues to be.<br /> As far as the movie goes, \\"Schindler's List\\" is absolute good. When Itzhak Stern said that the list is life, the same can be said for the film. \\"Schindler's List\\" is life.re shown the second shots of color (the first being the very beginning of the film with a family celebrating the Sabbath) as we see the \\"Schindler Jews\\" in present-day place rocks on Schindler's grave. 0307956350|2.0|I excitedly anticipated getting my hands on The Expat by Chris Pavone.  A well reviewed first novel from an author with an interesting pedigree and a strong female protagonist in an espionage thriller sounded like just the thing to add to my summer reading list. The protagonist, Kate, is a former CIA operative who has given up the excitement of life in the field for comforts of home, husband and children.  She has however neglected to tell her husband Dexter about her secret life.  When she finds he too may have sinister secrets she uses her skills as an operative to learn a version of the truth. It sounds great. I was, however, immediately disappointed with The Expats.  The numerous time shifts explaining back story and character actions were confusing.  Showing these things through dialogue would have been much more effective and smoothing out the timeline would make for a much more readable narrative. Perhaps the most disturbing, even jarring aspect of this work is the overly wordy nature of the prose.  I found myself thinking the author was trying to impress by showing how many arcane or multisyllabic words he knew or was trying for the world record in sentence length. Other reviewers have given examples of this tendency in The Expat and I found it immediately annoying. It necessitated reading and rereading to find out what the author was actually saying. I could not decide if the male author felt this overboard composition was the way women think, as the protagonist is female or he was just showing off.<br />The plot of The Expats ,as expected, was complex. There were many twists and turns.  Most were predicable, however, masked in the above referenced wordy prose.  The book could be a page-turner on the strength of the plot and its many twists but the writing style detracted from this powerful aspect of the story. The convoluted plot is made clear only in the final chapter with the tried and true method used by Hercule Perriot and others.  The main characters are assembled, review the evidence to reveal the true master criminal.  As a tense thriller The Expats fails to launch as its conclusion is obvious early in the second act and tricking the archvillian into revealing dialogue is a tired mystery device.<br />Where the story does excel however is conveying a feeling of alienation, the personal baggage of deceit and the toll secrets take on a person and a relationship.  This is a story with many layers. The setting and plot contribute to the overall alienation of the protagonist and help the reader understand her story. Unfortunately The Expats falls short mired in its own words.are assembled, review the evidence to reveal the true master criminal. Likeable Social Media was the first book of five I purchased in the business and social media field.<br /><br />Mr. Kerpen is a talented writer and complements his discussions about what it means to be a \\"likeable\\" brand with positive and negative anecdotes. It's a fairly quick read with frequent content headers, screenshots from Facebook and easily-digestible highlights to share with coworkers (such as the \\"10 Reasons Why Consumers Like Fan Pages on Facebook).<br /><br />There are other books and resources out there that give more concrete how-to's and a step-by-step guide to undertaking social media. While you could place this book in the hands of a CEO to show them the power of social media, I as a 21-year-old digital native confident in my knowledge of social media was able to walk away with roughly 10 pages of notes and suggestions.<br /><br />Likeable Social Media left me not only encouraged but empowered to undertake the creation of a social media strategy for a company.<br /><br />Where this book falls short is a lack of diversity. B004ZRV3AU|4.0|I read a bunch of reviews of different bottle cutters before deciding to buy this one. I wanted to recycle my stockpile of wine and soda bottles into juice glasses and wedding centerpiece vases.<br /><br />This bottle cutter works really well, though I don't think the hammer is great. I haven't had any luck with it. Instead I heat up a kettle, score the bottle once, dunk it in a bowl of cold water, then slowly pour the hot water over the score mark. Some thicker bottles take a couple rounds of hot/cold to break. Try to pour the hot water evenly over the score (keep turning the bottle) so the stress isn't focused on one section of glass - this can cause cracks. It definitely takes practice. I asked all my friends and neighbors to save their wine bottles for me. If you do get cracks you can hide them by painting or frosting the bottle.

No, I don't like all that Bradbury wrote, but his whimsical, lyrical style always attracted me.  He could create a world of \\"Firemen\\" in Fahrenheit 451 or the mysterious characters of \\"The Illustrated Man\\" and leave me entranced.<br /><br />The Martian Chronicles was no exception!<br /><br />First Impressions:<br /><br />The book itself is a loosely-knit series of short stories, one leading to the next, in date order in the writer's 21st century future.  Here we have Earth that is looking towards Mars as a haven from the brewing atomic wars and rumors of such.  What impressed me was the stylized characters and fleshed-out civilizations and how both Martian and Earthman deal with each other, as well as their own jealousies and prejudices.<br /><br />Stories!<br /><br />I won't bore the reader with a mini-review of each tale, but the few that I really liked involved some of the crazy characters - one an off-kilter man, Spender, part of a crew from the Fourth Expedition, who didn't want to see Mars commercialized as he looked upon the dead Martian civilization (destroyed by Man's diseases - holy War of the Worlds!) and decides to kill off his own men and keep the planet pristine!  That plan does not go over well with Captain Wilder.  The darkness of the story and its clear criticism of colonialism were enticing to me.<br /><br />The other story I really liked involved the last colonists on Mars (the rest being called back to Earth because of atomic war) who missed the last rocket, and gets lonely.  Far off, he hears a phone ring.  He finally finds who rang it, hoping for some female company, but the guy isn't so lonely that he does not have standards!<br /><br />Finally, the tale of a Martian and an Earth worker, both going to a party driving in their respective vehicles and meet each other on a lonely road - 10,000 years apart!  Crazy.<br /><br />Bottom Line:  Most of the stories flow well one to the other.  Ray does reflect some of the 1940s' style prejudices of the time which may put off modern readers, but if you read Ray's poetic style in its historic context, you too will see that a lot of his criticism and satire is still quite relevant.<br /><br />Highly recommended!><br />Bottom Line:  Most of the stories flow well one to the other. I think a lot of the venom directed toward the book comes from parents who secretly wonder whether Chua is right, and whether a super-strict, authoritarian parenting style is the best for their kids. It took me more than a year to pick up the book because I harbored this latent fear as well, but the book didn't move me from my parenting instincts. If anything, the lesson from Chua's narrative is that no two kids are the same, and the art of parenting is learning the specific methods that develop and motivate each of your children. Despite the initial marketing, Chua willingly relates her struggle to adapt her parenting style for her second daughter, and ends up backing away from her tigerness. This makes for a tension-filled, gripping narrative.<br /><br />I found Chua's writing to be a delight - full of irony and humor, precise and concise, making the book a quick and entertaining read. It was a clear five-star book for me until the ending, which I found too abrupt, with too many loose ends. The book's climax causes a profound change in Chua's philosophy, but she only gives us 20 pages of post-epiphany text versus the 210 pages she spends building up her pro-\\"Chinese parenting\\" argument and persona. I was uncertain what Chua wanted to take away from the book and wished she'd given us a better idea of her current thinking. Nevertheless, I applaud her for summoning the courage to open her family's story to the world and put into writing her innermost parenting struggles. She clearly knew how much opprobrium her initial parenting approach would create, since her daughter Lulu articulates all of this in the book already. I recommend that all readers give this book a chance - it is provocative in the best sense of the word.

B005I735F2|5.0|Electric Light Orchestra (ELO) were a British rock group from Birmingham, U.K., who released eleven studio albums, in the span of their music career, between 1971 & 2001. Their music is extremely creative and innovative - when I was a child, I always associated their music as something associated with the sci-fi genre & with the preconceived, supposed existence of aliens....this was due to the fact that the cover design of their album, \\"Out of the Blue\\" is an expressive, fantastic bolt out of the blue - released in an era where 'synthpop' was neither known nor too much appreciated by the world audience. Thus, Electric Light Orchestra is certainly the world's readiest candidate for the 'Most Grossly Misinterpreted And Most Unjustly Despised' band of the past thirty years - which is a great pity because they were highly talented & have representated their very own genre of rock music.<br /><br />Synthpop is  music that exhibits a predominance of the synthesizer, also electronic & bass guitars, accompanied by drums & an orchestra for the background music. Any CD of ELO has a sound quality that is always a bit muffled & blurry around the edges - this is part of how they want their music to sound & to the best of my knowledge, probaly done purposely to cast a unique blend of their own rock culture. The tone of the albums is almost always sharp - the treble is a bit hyped & the bass is toned down, to avoid a thumping drum beat - that is ELO for you!<br /><br />This album is positively the best you could posibly possess of all the ELO albums - it is the definitive/ultimate collection that is very indicative of all ELO albums -it is absolutely superb and good value for money for anyone who enjoys any variety of rock music. You would do well to go for it - in a rush!

B000005E60|2.0|The Sinfonia is one of Boulez's worst recorded performances. He simply has no gut-level feel for this delightful music, for its flambouyant theatricality. Plus the recording is poorly balanced, with crucial vocal and instrumental parts almost inaudible in places--especially in the centerpiece, the extraordinary Mahler/Beckett-trope movement.<br />The only truly recommendable commercial recording of the Sinfonia remains the 1968 performance by the original Swingle Singers and NYPO, all conducted by the composer. Since it's only the 4-movement version of the piece, it will likely never be released in digital transfer, and that's a crime.<br />The Eotvos is okay, but nothing special. Sound's a bit clearer than on the Boulez, but otherwise just a B/B- rendition. The Boulez gets a D/D+, and really just for the effort of making the first digital recording. But you gotta wonder why they bothered...<br />If you want to hear a great performance of this great piece, get the 1968 analog LP recording!

Before I bought this, I read a number of reviews stating that the Kindle Fire didn't fit, or that the cover didn't close over the Fire completely.<br />Concerning the fit, it is very snug, but I don't think that's a negative attribute. I don't want to worry about my tablet falling out, and the elastic bands keep it in tight. Also, I was wary about the elastic losing it's elasticity over time, but these bands are sewn securely into the inner cover, and aren't made of cheap elastic.<br />As far as the cover not laying flat on the face of the Fire, that's solely due to it being new. I remedied that in about two minutes. Here's the trick. Before you put your Fire in, hold the case with two hands (left hand on the top cover, right on the back), so it is open and facing you like an open book. Then, gingerly roll/rotate the spine back and forth, by pushing/pulling the front and back covers alternately. In about a minute or two, it will be broken in, and the top cover will lie neatly on the face of your Fire.

B005LAIHXQ|4.0|It has been over thirty years since director Ridley Scott (Gladiator, Black Hawk Down) made two highly influential, game-changing science fiction films, Alien and Blade Runner.  Now he revisits his earliest triumph, Alien, not as a sequel but as a kind of prequel that has threads connecting both films.  An intelligent science fiction film with elements of horror, Prometheus embraces bold concepts of human origin and its creation by `ancient astronauts'.  The film is not quite a classic, but should thoroughly satisfy purists and general moviegoers alike.<br /><br />An archeological couple has pieced together remarkable clues from early human drawings revealing a stellar map that may lead to the source of mankind.  The couple, Elizabeth Shaw (Noomi Rapace) and Charlie Holloway (Logan Marshall-Green), join a crew and its Captain Janek (Idris Elba) on a two year mission to a mysterious planet aboard the spaceship Prometheus.  Financed by the Weyland Corporation and represented by Meredith Vickers (Charlize Theron), the only non-human on board is an android named David (Michael Fassbender).  Prometheus lands near some structures which, upon investigation, reveal evidence of life, but the discovery has startling implications leading to a desperate struggle for survival that threatens the earth.<br /><br />The film is not without some surprises and plot twists, and without giving too much away, Scott likes to end his films with an ambiguous sense of hope.  There are some unanswered questions, however, and there is one glaring injustice that gets lost amid the film's fascinating conclusion.  One pivotal, incredible scene involves a remarkable, graphic emergency surgery that strains the limits of credibility, but nevertheless holds the viewers' agonized attention.<br /><br />The story has a compelling setup and when the crew goes inside the structure, they discover a series of chambers that takes the journey closer to potential answers or deadly threat.  It's like the ultimate funhouse of terrors.  It's also a safe bet that nasty things happen when curious humans meet invasive, alien life forms.<br /><br />Having to live up to its predecessor, Prometheus builds its own fascinating world and broadens storyline implications for future films. It's not Alien, whose novelty and groundbreaking creature designs stunned audiences, but there are certainly enough connections to draw parallels between the two films. Alien is referenced in several ways including bits of dialogue, a sampling of Jerry Goldsmith's original theme, and culminating with markedly familiar set pieces.  (Ironically Alien itself was a big budget reworking of a B-movie, It the Terror from Beyond Space.)<br /><br />The film does feel a bit derivative at times and borrows liberally from other science fiction classics.  The early scenes where David is the only conscious crew member who wiles away his solitary existence with basketball, watching holographic dreams of the hibernating crew, and admiring Lawrence of Arabia, harken back to the Discovery crew of Stanley Kubrick's 2001: A Space Odyssey.  Some scenes are not original as they seem lifted right out of Alien vs. Predator, Leviathan and various episodes of TV's The X Files.<br /><br />Fassbender, who shines in the film, does a very convincing job imbuing his android with almost human characteristics and a potentially sinister side.  Think of 2001's disembodied computer HAL and you get the idea.  Strong female leads are a staple of Scott's films (Thelma and Louise), and Rapace is a strong female heroine whose faith is sorely tested amid personal loss and her obsessive hunt for the truth about mankind's beginnings.<br /><br />Cinematography has always been a strong point in Scott's films, and the film's stunning visuals display the planet's otherworldly beauty.  The production design is imaginative particularly inside the structure with its myriad of biomechanical architecture and impressive special effects.  Swiss artist H.R. Giger, who designed the original Alien monster, contributes a bit of reverse engineering that fans will appreciate.<br /><br />Prometheus is a thought provoking adventure that not only can stand on its own, but rises tall among most science fiction films.  It's a visually impressive epic with an ambitious agenda that doesn't completely work, but even so, this antecedent to Alien is a worthy bookend to the series.
